	<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Diwali</h1>
	
	<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
		<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
		<div id="contentSub"></div>
		
		
		
		<div id="jump-to-nav"></div>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
		<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
		<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">"Deepavali" and "Dipawali" redirect here. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Deepavali_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Deepavali (disambiguation)">Deepavali (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Hindu festival of lights</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">

</p>
<table class="infobox vevent" style="width:22em"><tbody><tr><th colspan="2" class="hd" style="text-align:center;font-size:125%;font-weight:bold;background-color: orange">Diwali</th></tr><tr><td href="/wiki/Rangoli" title="Rangoli">Rangoli</a> decorations, made using coloured fine powder or sand, are popular during Diwali.</div></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Also called</th><td>Deepavali</td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Observed&#160;by</th><td><a href="/wiki/Hindus" title="Hindus">Hindus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jains" class="mw-redirect" title="Jains">Jains</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sikhs" title="Sikhs">Sikhs</a>, and some <a href="/wiki/Buddhists" class="mw-redirect" title="Buddhists">Buddhists</a> (notably <a href="/wiki/Newar_Buddhism" title="Newar Buddhism">Newar Buddhists</a>)<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Type</th><td>Cultural, seasonal, religious</td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Celebrations</th><td><a href="/wiki/Diya_(light)" class="mw-redirect" title="Diya (light)">Diya</a> and lighting, home decoration, shopping, fireworks, <a href="/wiki/Puja_(Hinduism)" title="Puja (Hinduism)">puja</a> (worship ceremonies), gifts, performing religious rituals, feast and sweets</td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Date</th><td>Varies per <a href="/wiki/Hindu_calendar" title="Hindu calendar">Hindu calendar</a></td></tr><tr><th scope="row">2019&#160;date</th><td>October
<p>25th(<a href="/wiki/Dhanteras" title="Dhanteras">Dhanteras</a>)
26th(<a href="/wiki/Naraka_Chaturdashi" title="Naraka Chaturdashi">Naraka Chaturdashi</a>)
27th(<a href="/wiki/Lakshmi_Puja" title="Lakshmi Puja">Lakshmi Puja</a>)
</p>
28th(<a href="/wiki/Balipratipada" title="Balipratipada">Balipratipada</a>/<a href="/wiki/Bhai_Dooj" title="Bhai Dooj">Bhai Dooj</a>)</td></tr><tr><th scope="row">Related&#160;to</th><td><a href="/wiki/Kali_Puja" title="Kali Puja">Kali Puja</a>, <a href="/wiki/Galungan" title="Galungan">Galungan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Diwali_(Jainism)" title="Diwali (Jainism)">Diwali (Jainism)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bandi_Chhor_Divas" title="Bandi Chhor Divas">Bandi Chhor Divas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tihar_(festival)" title="Tihar (festival)">Tihar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Swanti_(festival)" title="Swanti (festival)">Swanti</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sohrai" title="Sohrai">Sohrai</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bandna" title="Bandna">Bandna</a></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Diwali</b>, <b>Deepavali</b> or <b>Dipavali</b> is a four to five day-long (varying as per Hindu Calendar) festival of lights, which is celebrated by <a href="/wiki/Hindus" title="Hindus">Hindus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jains" class="mw-redirect" title="Jains">Jains</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sikhs" title="Sikhs">Sikhs</a> and some <a href="/wiki/Buddhists" class="mw-redirect" title="Buddhists">Buddhists</a> every autumn in the northern hemisphere (spring in southern hemisphere).<sup id="cite_ref-OED-Diwali_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OED-Diwali-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> One of the most popular festivals of <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a>, Diwali symbolises the spiritual "victory of light over darkness, good over evil and knowledge over ignorance." Light is a metaphor for knowledge and consciousness.<sup id="cite_ref-Heiligman31_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Heiligman31-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-htoday_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-htoday-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> During the celebration, temples, homes, shops and office buildings are brightly illuminated.<sup id="cite_ref-fs_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fs-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> The preparations, and rituals, for the festival typically last five days, with the climax occurring on the third day coinciding with the darkest night of the <a href="/wiki/Hindu_calendar" title="Hindu calendar">Hindu lunisolar</a> month <a href="/wiki/Kartik_(month)" title="Kartik (month)">Kartika</a>. In the <a href="/wiki/Gregorian_calendar" title="Gregorian calendar">Gregorian calendar</a>, the festival generally falls between mid-October and mid-November.<sup id="cite_ref-melton252_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-melton252-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the lead-up to Diwali, celebrants will prepare by cleaning, renovating, and decorating their homes and workplaces.<sup id="cite_ref-Raat_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Raat-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> During the climax, revellers adorn themselves in their finest clothes, illuminate the interior and exterior of their homes with <i><a href="/wiki/Diya_(lamp)" title="Diya (lamp)">diyas</a></i> (oil lamps or candles), offer <i>puja</i> (worship) to <a href="/wiki/Lakshmi" title="Lakshmi">Lakshmi</a>, the goddess of prosperity and wealth,<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;note 1&#93;</a></sup> light fireworks, and partake in family feasts, where <i>mithai</i> (<a href="/wiki/South_Asian_sweets" class="mw-redirect" title="South Asian sweets">sweets</a>) and gifts are shared. Diwali is also a major cultural event for the Hindu, Sikh, Jain, and Buddhist diaspora from the Indian subcontinent.<sup id="cite_ref-wsj_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wsj-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHenry_Johnson200771–73_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHenry_Johnson200771–73-14">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKelly198840–55_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKelly198840–55-15">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The five-day festival originated in the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a> and is mentioned in early <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a> texts. The names of the festive days of Diwali, documented by Qa Kishore, as well as the rituals, vary by region. Diwali is usually celebrated eighteen days after the <a href="/wiki/Navrati" class="mw-redirect" title="Navrati">Dussehra</a> (Dasara, Dasain) festival, with <a href="/wiki/Dhanteras" title="Dhanteras">Dhanteras</a>, or the regional equivalent, marking the first day of the festival when celebrants prepare by cleaning their homes and making decorations on the floor, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Rangoli" title="Rangoli">rangoli</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> The second day is <a href="/wiki/Naraka_Chaturdashi" title="Naraka Chaturdashi">Naraka Chaturdashi</a>, or the regional equivalent which for Hindus in the south of India is Diwali proper. Western, central, eastern and northern Indian communities observe main day of Diwali on the third day i.e. the day of Lakshmi Puja and the darkest night of the traditional month. In some parts of India, the day after Lakshmi Puja is marked with the <i>Govardhan Puja and <a href="/wiki/Balipratipada" title="Balipratipada">Balipratipada</a>(Padwa)</i>, which is dedicated to the relationship between wife and husband. Some Hindu communities mark the last day as <a href="/wiki/Bhai_Dooj" title="Bhai Dooj">Bhai Dooj</a> or the regional equivalent, which is dedicated to the bond between sister and brother,<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> while other Hindu and Sikh craftsmen communities mark this day as <i><a href="/wiki/Vishwakarma_Puja" title="Vishwakarma Puja">Vishwakarma Puja</a></i> and observe it by performing maintenance in their work spaces and offering prayers.<sup id="cite_ref-haar2009_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-haar2009-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Some other faiths in India also celebrate their respective festivals alongside Diwali. The <a href="/wiki/Jainism" title="Jainism">Jains</a> observe their own Diwali, which marks the final liberation of <a href="/wiki/Mahavira" title="Mahavira">Mahavira</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Sharma2006_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sharma2006-20">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-N._Upadhye,_Review_1982_pp._231-232_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-N._Upadhye,_Review_1982_pp._231-232-21">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Sikhism" title="Sikhism">Sikhs</a> celebrate <a href="/wiki/Bandi_Chhor_Divas" title="Bandi Chhor Divas">Bandi Chhor Divas</a> to mark the release of Guru <a href="/wiki/Hargobind" class="mw-redirect" title="Hargobind">Hargobind</a> from a <a href="/wiki/Mughal_Empire" title="Mughal Empire">Mughal Empire</a> prison,<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> while <a href="/wiki/Newar_Buddhism" title="Newar Buddhism">Newar Buddhists</a>, unlike other Buddhists, celebrate Diwali by worshipping Lakshmi, while the <a href="/wiki/Bengali_Hindus" title="Bengali Hindus">Bengali Hindus</a> generally celebrate Diwali, by worshipping Goddess <a href="/wiki/Kali" title="Kali">Kali</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-diwaliBuddhist_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-diwaliBuddhist-23">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Prem_Saran_2012_175_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Prem_Saran_2012_175-24">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> The main day of the festival of Diwali i.e the day of Lakshmi Puja is an official holiday in <a href="/wiki/Fiji" title="Fiji">Fiji</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Guyana" title="Guyana">Guyana</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malaysia" title="Malaysia">Malaysia</a> (except Sarawak),<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mauritius" title="Mauritius">Mauritius</a>, <a href="/wiki/Myanmar" title="Myanmar">Myanmar</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Nepal" title="Nepal">Nepal</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Singapore" title="Singapore">Singapore</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka" title="Sri Lanka">Sri Lanka</a>, <a href="/wiki/Suriname" title="Suriname">Suriname</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Trinidad_and_Tobago" title="Trinidad and Tobago">Trinidad and Tobago</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Nomenclature_and_dates">Nomenclature and dates</span></h2>

The festivities vary between different regions.<sup id="cite_ref-fs_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fs-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-tp_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tp-32">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-dhcd_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dhcd-33">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><i>Diwali</i> (<span class="nowrap"><small>English:  </small><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="&#39;d&#39; in &#39;dye&#39;">d</span><span title="/ɪ/: &#39;i&#39; in &#39;kit&#39;">ɪ</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="&#39;w&#39; in &#39;wind&#39;">w</span><span title="/ɑː/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;father&#39;">ɑː</span><span title="&#39;l&#39; in &#39;lie&#39;">l</span><span title="/iː/: &#39;ee&#39; in &#39;fleece&#39;">iː</span></span>/</a></span></span>)<sup id="cite_ref-OED-Diwali_2-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OED-Diwali-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> or <i>Divali</i><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECybelle_T._Shattuck199951,_124_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECybelle_T._Shattuck199951,_124-34">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> is from the <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a> <i>dīpāvali</i> meaning "row or series of lights".<sup id="cite_ref-jgl_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jgl-35">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJessica_FrazierGavin_Flood2011255_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJessica_FrazierGavin_Flood2011255-36">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> The conjugated term is derived from the Sanskrit words <i>dīpa</i>, "lamp, light, lantern, candle, that which glows, shines, illuminates or knowledge"<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> and <i>āvali</i>, "a row, range, continuous line, series".<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;note 2&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The five-day celebration is observed every year in early autumn after the conclusion of the summer harvest and coincides with the new moon, known as the <i>amāsvasya</i> – the darkest night of the <a href="/wiki/Hindu_calendar" title="Hindu calendar">Hindu lunisolar calendar</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETracy_Pintchman200561–62_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETracy_Pintchman200561–62-40">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> The festivities begin two days before <i>amāsvasya</i>, on Dhanteras, and extends two days after, the second day of the first fortnight of the month of Kartik.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETracy_Pintchman200561_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETracy_Pintchman200561-41">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup> According to Indologist, Constance Jones who specialises in religious sociology, this night ends the lunar month of Ashwin and starts the month of Kartika.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEConstance_Jones2011252–53_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConstance_Jones2011252–53-42">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;note 3&#93;</a></sup> The darkest night is the apex of the celebration and coincides with the second half of October or early November in the Gregorian calendar.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEConstance_Jones2011252–53_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConstance_Jones2011252–53-42">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The festival climax is on the third day and is called the main Diwali. It is an official holiday in about a dozen countries, while the other festive days are regionally observed as either public or optional restricted holidays in <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-about_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-about-45">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Nepal" title="Nepal">Nepal</a>, it is also a multiday festival, although the days and rituals are named differently, with the climax being called the <i><a href="/wiki/Tihar_(festival)" title="Tihar (festival)">Tihar</a></i> festival by Hindus and <i><a href="/wiki/Swanti_(festival)" title="Swanti (festival)">Swanti</a></i> festival by Buddhists.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>
<p>The Diwali festival is likely a fusion of harvest festivals in ancient India.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEConstance_Jones2011252–53_42-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConstance_Jones2011252–53-42">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> It is mentioned in Sanskrit texts such as the <i><a href="/wiki/Padma_Purana" title="Padma Purana">Padma Purana</a></i>, the <i><a href="/wiki/Skanda_Purana" title="Skanda Purana">Skanda Purana</a></i> both of which were completed in the second half of the 1st millennium CE. The <i>diyas</i> (lamps) are mentioned in Skanda Kishore Purana as symbolising parts of the sun, describing it as the cosmic giver of light and energy to all life and which seasonally transitions in the Hindu calendar month of Kartik.<sup id="cite_ref-tp_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tp-32">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJames_G._Lochtefeld2002355_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJames_G._Lochtefeld2002355-48">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>King Harsha refers to Deepavali, in the 7th century Sanskrit play <i><a href="/wiki/Nagananda" title="Nagananda">Nagananda</a></i>, as <i>Dīpapratipadotsava</i> (<i>dīpa</i> = light, <i>pratipadā</i> = first day, <i>utsava</i> = festival), where lamps were lit and newly engaged brides and grooms received gifts.<sup id="cite_ref-bnsharma_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bnsharma-49">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-yaksha_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-yaksha-50">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Rajashekhara_(Sanskrit_poet)" title="Rajashekhara (Sanskrit poet)">Rajasekhara</a> referred to Deepavali as <i>Dipamalika</i> in his 9th century <i>Kavyamimamsa</i>, wherein he mentions the tradition of homes being whitewashed and oil lamps decorated homes, streets and markets in the night.<sup id="cite_ref-bnsharma_49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bnsharma-49">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Diwali was also described by numerous travellers from outside India. In his 11th century memoir on India, the Persian traveller and historian <a href="/wiki/Ab%C5%AB_Ray%E1%B8%A5%C4%81n_al-B%C4%ABr%C5%ABn%C4%AB" class="mw-redirect" title="Abū Rayḥān al-Bīrūnī">Al Biruni</a> wrote of Deepavali being celebrated by Hindus on the day of the New Moon in the month of Kartika.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> The Venetian merchant and traveller <a href="/wiki/Niccol%C3%B2_de%27_Conti" title="Niccolò de&#39; Conti">Niccolò de' Conti</a> visited India in the early 15th-century and wrote in his memoir, "on another of these festivals they fix up within their temples, and on the outside of the roofs, an innumerable number of oil lamps... which are kept burning day and night" and that the families would gather, "clothe themselves in new garments", sing, dance and feast.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAbraham_Eraly2015315–16_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAbraham_Eraly2015315–16-52">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobert_Sewell200685–86_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobert_Sewell200685–86-53">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> The 16th-century Portuguese traveller <a href="/wiki/Domingo_Paes" title="Domingo Paes">Domingo Paes</a> wrote of his visit to the Hindu <a href="/wiki/Vijayanagara_Empire" title="Vijayanagara Empire">Vijayanagara Empire</a>, where <i>Dipavali</i> was celebrated in October with householders illuminating their homes, and their temples, with lamps.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobert_Sewell200685–86_53-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobert_Sewell200685–86-53">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Islamic historians of the Delhi Sultanate and the Mughal Empire era also mentioned Diwali and other Hindu festivals. A few, notably the <a href="/wiki/Mughal_Empire" title="Mughal Empire">Mughal</a> emperor <a href="/wiki/Akbar" title="Akbar">Akbar</a>, welcomed and participated in the festivities,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERichard_M._Eaton1996159–60_with_footnotes_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERichard_M._Eaton1996159–60_with_footnotes-54">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECharles_Melville2012526,_Quote:_&quot;He_&#91;Mahmud_b._Amir_Vali&#93;_gives_a_very_detailed_account_of_the_celebration_of_the_ten_days_of_Moharram,_which_he_witnessed_in_Lahore_in_1965,_as_well_as_Hindu_festivals_such_as_Diwali_(...)&quot;._55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECharles_Melville2012526,_Quote:_&quot;He_[Mahmud_b._Amir_Vali]_gives_a_very_detailed_account_of_the_celebration_of_the_ten_days_of_Moharram,_which_he_witnessed_in_Lahore_in_1965,_as_well_as_Hindu_festivals_such_as_Diwali_(...)&quot;.-55">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> whereas others banned such festivals as Diwali and <a href="/wiki/Holi" title="Holi">Holi</a>, as <a href="/wiki/Aurangzeb" title="Aurangzeb">Aurangzeb</a> did in 1665.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKiyokazu_Okita201428–29_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKiyokazu_Okita201428–29-56">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStephen_Blake201387–89_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStephen_Blake201387–89-57">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;note 4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;note 5&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Publications from the British colonial era also made mention of Diwali, such as the note on Hindu festivals published in 1799 by <a href="/wiki/William_Jones_(philologist)" title="William Jones (philologist)">Sir William Jones</a>, a philologist known for his early observations on <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a> and <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">Indo-European languages</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-jone1799p263_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jone1799p263-62">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> In his paper on <i>The Lunar Year of the Hindus</i>, Jones, then based in <a href="/wiki/Bengal" title="Bengal">Bengal</a>, noted four of the five days of Diwali in the autumn months of <i>Aswina-Cartica</i> [sic] as the following: <i>Bhutachaturdasi Yamaterpanam</i> (2nd day), <i>Lacshmipuja dipanwita</i> (the day of Diwali), <i>Dyuta pratipat Belipuja</i> (4th day), and <i>Bhratri dwitiya</i> (5th day). The <i>Lacshmipuja dipanwita</i>, remarked Jones, was a "great festival at night, in honor of Lakshmi, with illuminations on trees and houses".<sup id="cite_ref-jone1799p263_62-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jone1799p263-62">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;note 6&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Epigraphy">Epigraphy</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1867_CE_chromolithograph,_Diwali,_feast_of_lamps,_by_William_Simpson.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/1867_CE_chromolithograph%2C_Diwali%2C_feast_of_lamps%2C_by_William_Simpson.jpg/220px-1867_CE_chromolithograph%2C_Diwali%2C_feast_of_lamps%2C_by_William_Simpson.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="154" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/1867_CE_chromolithograph%2C_Diwali%2C_feast_of_lamps%2C_by_William_Simpson.jpg/330px-1867_CE_chromolithograph%2C_Diwali%2C_feast_of_lamps%2C_by_William_Simpson.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/1867_CE_chromolithograph%2C_Diwali%2C_feast_of_lamps%2C_by_William_Simpson.jpg/440px-1867_CE_chromolithograph%2C_Diwali%2C_feast_of_lamps%2C_by_William_Simpson.jpg 2x" data-file-width="712" data-file-height="500" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1867_CE_chromolithograph,_Diwali,_feast_of_lamps,_by_William_Simpson.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>William Simpson labelled his chromolithograph of 1867 CE as "Dewali, feast of lamps". It showed streets lit up at dusk, with a girl and her mother lighting a street corner lamp.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Sanskrit inscriptions in stone and copper mentioning Diwali, occasionally alongside terms such as <i>Dipotsava</i>, <i>Dipavali</i>, <i>Divali</i> and <i>Divalige</i>, have been discovered at numerous sites across India.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;note 7&#93;</a></sup> Examples include a 10th-century Rashtrakuta empire copper plate inscription of Krsna III (939–967 CE) that mentions <i>Dipotsava</i>,<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> and a 12th-century mixed Sanskrit-Kannada Sinda inscription discovered in the Isvara temple of <a href="/wiki/Dharwad" title="Dharwad">Dharwad</a> in Karnataka where the inscription refers to the festival as a "sacred occasion".<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Lorenz_Franz_Kielhorn" title="Lorenz Franz Kielhorn">Lorenz Franz Kielhorn</a>, a German Indologist known for translating many Indic inscriptions, this festival is mentioned as <i>Dipotsavam</i> in verses 6 and 7 of the Ranganatha temple Sanskrit inscription of the 13th-century <a href="/wiki/Kerala" title="Kerala">Kerala</a> Hindu king Ravivarman Samgramadhira. Part of the inscription, as translated by Kielhorn, reads: "the auspicious festival of lights which disperses the most profound darkness, which in former days was celebrated by the kings Ila, Kartavirya and Sagara, (...) as Sakra (Indra) is of the gods, the universal monarch who knows the duties by the three Vedas, afterwards celebrated here at Ranga for Vishnu, resplendent with Lakshmi resting on his radiant lap."<sup id="cite_ref-kielhorn1896_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kielhorn1896-69">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;note 8&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Jain inscriptions, such as the 10th-century Saundatti inscription about a donation of oil to Jinendra worship for the Diwali rituals, speak of <i>Dipotsava</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> Another early 13th-century Sanskrit stone inscription, written in the Devanagari script, has been found in the north end of a mosque pillar in <a href="/wiki/Jalore" title="Jalore">Jalore</a>, Rajasthan evidently built using materials from a demolished Jain temple. The inscription states that Ramachandracharya built and dedicated a drama performance hall, with a golden cupola, on Diwali.<sup id="cite_ref-Hultzsch1268_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hultzsch1268-73">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;note 9&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
