<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Glossary_of_Hinduism_terms" title="Glossary of Hinduism terms">Glossary of Hinduism terms</a></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/File:Aum_Om_red.svg" class="image"><img alt="Aum Om red.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Aum_Om_red.svg/16px-Aum_Om_red.svg.png" decoding="async" width="16" height="16" class="noviewer" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Aum_Om_red.svg/23px-Aum_Om_red.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Aum_Om_red.svg/31px-Aum_Om_red.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="356" data-file-height="367" /></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Hinduism" title="Portal:Hinduism">Hinduism&#32;portal</a></i> <a href="/wiki/File:HinduSwastika.svg" class="image"><img alt="HinduSwastika.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/HinduSwastika.svg/15px-HinduSwastika.svg.png" decoding="async" width="15" height="15" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/HinduSwastika.svg/23px-HinduSwastika.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/HinduSwastika.svg/30px-HinduSwastika.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="142" data-file-height="145" /></a></li></ul></td></tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Hinduism" title="Template:Hinduism"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Hinduism" title="Template talk:Hinduism"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Hinduism&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Diwali is celebrated by Hindus, Jains, Sikhs, and Newar Buddhists,<sup id="cite_ref-diwaliBuddhist_23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-diwaliBuddhist-23">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> although for each faith it marks different historical events and stories, but nonetheless the festival represents the same symbolic victory of light over darkness, knowledge over ignorance, and good over evil.<sup id="cite_ref-Heiligman31_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Heiligman31-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-htoday_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-htoday-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hinduism">Hinduism</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Raja_Ravi_Varma,_Goddess_Lakshmi,_1896.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Raja_Ravi_Varma%2C_Goddess_Lakshmi%2C_1896.jpg/220px-Raja_Ravi_Varma%2C_Goddess_Lakshmi%2C_1896.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="311" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Raja_Ravi_Varma%2C_Goddess_Lakshmi%2C_1896.jpg/330px-Raja_Ravi_Varma%2C_Goddess_Lakshmi%2C_1896.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Raja_Ravi_Varma%2C_Goddess_Lakshmi%2C_1896.jpg/440px-Raja_Ravi_Varma%2C_Goddess_Lakshmi%2C_1896.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1057" data-file-height="1496" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Raja_Ravi_Varma,_Goddess_Lakshmi,_1896.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Diwali is celebrated in the honour of <a href="/wiki/Lakshmi" title="Lakshmi">Lakshmi</a>, the goddess of wealth.</div></div></div>
<p>The religious significance of Diwali varies regionally within India. The festival is associated with a diversity of deities, traditions, and symbolism.<sup id="cite_ref-Heiligman31_4-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Heiligman31-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-vasudha31_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vasudha31-77">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJessica_FrazierGavin_Flood2011255_36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJessica_FrazierGavin_Flood2011255-36">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> These variations, states Constance Jones, may reflect diverse local autumn harvest festivals that fused into one pan-Hindu festival with a shared spiritual significance and ritual grammar while retaining local traditions.<sup id="cite_ref-melton252_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-melton252-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>One tradition links the festival to legends in the Hindu epic <i><a href="/wiki/Ramayana" title="Ramayana">Ramayana</a></i>, where Diwali is the day <a href="/wiki/Vishnu" title="Vishnu">Vishnu's</a> <a href="/wiki/Avatar" title="Avatar">avatar</a> <a href="/wiki/Rama" title="Rama">Rama</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lakshmi" title="Lakshmi">Lakshmi's</a> avatar <a href="/wiki/Sita" title="Sita">Sita</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shesha" title="Shesha">Shesha's</a> avatar <a href="/wiki/Lakshmana" title="Lakshmana">Lakshmana</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Shiva" title="Shiva">Shiva's</a> avatar <a href="/wiki/Hanuman" title="Hanuman">Hanuman</a> reached <a href="/wiki/Ayodhya" title="Ayodhya">Ayodhya</a> after a 14 year period in exile and Rama's army of good defeated the demon king <a href="/wiki/Ravana" title="Ravana">Ravana</a>'s army of evil in the <a href="/wiki/Treta_Yuga" title="Treta Yuga">Treta Yuga</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOm_Lata_Bahadur200691_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOm_Lata_Bahadur200691-78">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>As per another popular tradition, in the <a href="/wiki/Dwapara_Yuga" class="mw-redirect" title="Dwapara Yuga">Dwapara Yuga</a> Period, <a href="/wiki/Lord_Vishnu" class="mw-redirect" title="Lord Vishnu">Lord Vishnu</a> as incarnation of <a href="/wiki/Krishna" title="Krishna">Krishna</a> killed the Demon <a href="/wiki/Narakasura" title="Narakasura">Narakasura</a>, who was evil king of Pragjyotishapura, near present-day Assam and released 16000 girls captivated by Narakasura. Diwali was celebrated as a significance of triumph of good over evil after Lord Krishna's Victory over Narakasura. The day before Diwali is remembered as Naraka Chaturdasi, the day on which Narakasura was killed by Lord Krishna.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Many Hindus associate the festival with <a href="/wiki/Lakshmi" title="Lakshmi">Lakshmi</a>, the goddess of wealth and prosperity, and wife of <a href="/wiki/Vishnu" title="Vishnu">Vishnu</a>. According to Pintchman, the start of the 5-day Diwali festival is stated in some popular contemporary sources as the day Goddess Lakshmi was born from <a href="/wiki/Samudra_manthan" title="Samudra manthan">Samudra manthan</a>, the churning of the cosmic ocean of milk by the <a href="/wiki/Deva_(Hinduism)" title="Deva (Hinduism)">Devas</a> (gods) and the <a href="/wiki/Asuras" class="mw-redirect" title="Asuras">Asuras</a> (demons) – a Vedic legend that is also found in several <a href="/wiki/Puranas" title="Puranas">Puranas</a> such as the <i><a href="/wiki/Padma_Purana" title="Padma Purana">Padma Purana</a></i>, while the night of Diwali is when Lakshmi chose and wed Vishnu.<sup id="cite_ref-tp_32-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tp-32">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Karen_Pechilis_2007_pp_273-275_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Karen_Pechilis_2007_pp_273-275-80">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> Along with Lakshmi, who is representative of <a href="/wiki/Vaishnavism" title="Vaishnavism">Vaishnavism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ganesha" title="Ganesha">Ganesha</a>, the elephant-headed son of <a href="/wiki/Parvati" title="Parvati">Parvati</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shiva" title="Shiva">Shiva</a> of <a href="/wiki/Shaivism" title="Shaivism">Shaivism</a> tradition, is remembered as one who symbolises ethical beginnings and the remover of obstacles.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOm_Lata_Bahadur200691_78-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOm_Lata_Bahadur200691-78">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Hindus of eastern India associate the festival with the goddess <a href="/wiki/Durga" title="Durga">Durga</a>, or her fierce avatar <a href="/wiki/Kali" title="Kali">Kali</a> (<a href="/wiki/Shaktism" title="Shaktism">Shaktism</a>), who symbolises the victory of good over evil.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELaura_Amazzone2012_10-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELaura_Amazzone2012-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> Hindus from the Braj region in northern India, parts of Assam, as well as southern Tamil and Telugu communities view Diwali as the day the god <a href="/wiki/Krishna" title="Krishna">Krishna</a> overcame and destroyed the evil demon king Narakasura, in yet another symbolic victory of knowledge and good over ignorance and evil.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMichael_D._Coogan2003152–53_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMichael_D._Coogan2003152–53-83">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELavanya_Vemsani2016190–91_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELavanya_Vemsani2016190–91-84">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Trade and merchant families and others also offer prayers to <a href="/wiki/Saraswati" title="Saraswati">Saraswati</a>, who embodies music, literature and learning and <a href="/wiki/Kubera" title="Kubera">Kubera</a>, who symbolises book-keeping, treasury and wealth management.<sup id="cite_ref-tp_32-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tp-32">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> In western states such as Gujarat, and certain northern Hindu communities of India, the festival of Diwali signifies the start of a new year.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMichael_D._Coogan2003152–53_83-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMichael_D._Coogan2003152–53-83">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Mythical tales shared on Diwali vary widely depending on region and even within Hindu tradition,<sup id="cite_ref-vasudha31_77-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-vasudha31-77">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> yet all share a common focus on righteousness, self-inquiry and the importance of knowledge,<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> which, according to Lindsey Harlan, an Indologist and scholar of Religious Studies, is the path to overcoming the "darkness of ignorance".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrank_Salamone2004112,_Article_on_Divali_by_Lindsey_Harlan_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrank_Salamone2004112,_Article_on_Divali_by_Lindsey_Harlan-87">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> The telling of these myths are a reminder of the Hindu belief that good ultimately triumphs over evil.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDarra_Goldstein2015222–23_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDarra_Goldstein2015222–23-89">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Jainism">Jainism</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Diwali_(Jainism)" title="Diwali (Jainism)">Diwali (Jainism)</a></div>
<p>Jeffrey Long, a scholar of Jain and Hindu studies, states that in Jain tradition, Diwali is celebrated in observance of "Mahavira Nirvana Divas", the physical death and final nirvana of <a href="/wiki/Mahavira" title="Mahavira">Mahavira</a>. The Jain Diwali celebrated in many parts of India has similar practices to the Hindu Diwali, such as the lighting of lamps and the offering of prayers to Lakshmi. However, the focus of the Jain Diwali remains the dedication to Mahavira.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJeffery_D._Long200926,_42_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJeffery_D._Long200926,_42-90">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> According to the Jain tradition, this practice of lighting lamps first began on the day of Mahavira's nirvana in 527 BCE,<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;note 10&#93;</a></sup> when 18 kings who had gathered for Mahavira's final teachings issued a proclamation that lamps be lit in remembrance of the "great light, Mahavira".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJ_Gordon_Melton2011255_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJ_Gordon_Melton2011255-94">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJeffery_D._Long200942_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJeffery_D._Long200942-95">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> This traditional belief of the origin of Diwali, and its significance to Jains, is reflected in their historic artworks such as paintings.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJyotindra_JainEberhard_Fischer197813_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJyotindra_JainEberhard_Fischer197813-96">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sikhism">Sikhism</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bandi_Chhor_Divas" title="Bandi Chhor Divas">Bandi Chhor Divas</a></div>
<p>Sikhs celebrate Bandi Chhor Divas in remembrance of the release of <a href="/wiki/Guru_Hargobind" title="Guru Hargobind">Guru Hargobind</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Gwalior_Fort" title="Gwalior Fort">Gwalior Fort</a> prison by the Mughal emperor, <a href="/wiki/Jahangir" title="Jahangir">Jahangir</a>, and the day he arrived at the Golden Temple in Amritsar.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> According to J.S. Grewal, a scholar of Sikhism and Sikh history, Diwali in the Sikh tradition is older than the sixth Guru Hargobind legend. <a href="/wiki/Guru_Amar_Das" title="Guru Amar Das">Guru Amar Das</a>, the third Guru of the Sikhs, built a well in Goindwal with eighty-four steps and invited Sikhs to bathe in its sacred waters on Baisakhi and Diwali as a form of community bonding. Over time, these spring and autumn festivals became the most important of Sikh festivals and holy sites such as Amritsar became focal points for annual pilgrimages.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJ._S._Grewal199850–51,_60,_73–78,_93_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJ._S._Grewal199850–51,_60,_73–78,_93-98">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> The festival of Diwali, according to Ray Colledge, highlights three events in Sikh history: the founding of the city of Amritsar in 1577, the release of Guru Hargobind from the Mughal prison, and the day of Bhai Mani Singh's martyrdom in 1738 as a result of his failure to pay a fine for trying to celebrate Diwali and thereafter refusing to convert to Islam.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERay_Colledge2017276–78_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERay_Colledge2017276–78-99">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPashaura_SinghLouis_E._Fenech2014431–33_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPashaura_SinghLouis_E._Fenech2014431–33-100">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;note 11&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Diwali celebrations of <a href="/wiki/Amritsar" title="Amritsar">Amritsar</a> is most famous in India. Nearly Millions of Devotees visit <a href="/wiki/Golden_Temple" title="Golden Temple">Golden temple</a> to celebrate diwali every year. <sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Buddhism">Buddhism</span></h3>
<p>Diwali is not a festival for most Buddhists, with the exception of the <a href="/wiki/Newar_people" title="Newar people">Newar</a> people of Nepal who revere various deities in the <a href="/wiki/Vajrayana" title="Vajrayana">Vajrayana</a> Buddhism and celebrate Diwali by offering prayers to Lakshmi.<sup id="cite_ref-diwaliBuddhist_23-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-diwaliBuddhist-23">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Prem_Saran_2012_175_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Prem_Saran_2012_175-24">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Newar Buddhists in Nepalese valleys also celebrate the Diwali festival over five days, in much the same way, and on the same days, as the Nepalese Hindu Diwali-Tihar festival.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> According to some observers, this traditional celebration by Newar Buddhists in Nepal, through the worship of Lakshmi and Vishnu during Diwali, is not <a href="/wiki/Syncretism" title="Syncretism">syncretism</a> but rather a reflection of the freedom within <a href="/wiki/Mahayana" title="Mahayana">Mahayana</a> Buddhist tradition to worship any deity for their worldly betterment.<sup id="cite_ref-diwaliBuddhist_23-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-diwaliBuddhist-23">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Description">Description</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Diwali_in_Sri_Lanka_Culture_and_Sights.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Diwali_in_Sri_Lanka_Culture_and_Sights.jpg/220px-Diwali_in_Sri_Lanka_Culture_and_Sights.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="168" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Diwali_in_Sri_Lanka_Culture_and_Sights.jpg/330px-Diwali_in_Sri_Lanka_Culture_and_Sights.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Diwali_in_Sri_Lanka_Culture_and_Sights.jpg/440px-Diwali_in_Sri_Lanka_Culture_and_Sights.jpg 2x" data-file-width="541" data-file-height="414" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Diwali_in_Sri_Lanka_Culture_and_Sights.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Diwali celebrations include <a href="/wiki/Puja_(Hinduism)" title="Puja (Hinduism)">puja</a> (prayers) to Lakshmi and Ganesha. Lakshmi is of the Vaishnavism tradition, while Ganesha of the Shaivism tradition of Hinduism.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOm_Lata_Bahadur200692–93_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOm_Lata_Bahadur200692–93-106">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavid_Kinsley198833–34_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavid_Kinsley198833–34-107">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Diwali is a five-day festival, the height of which is celebrated on the third day coinciding with the darkest night of the lunar month. During the festival, Hindus, Jains and Sikhs illuminate their homes, temples and work spaces with <a href="/wiki/Diya_(light)" class="mw-redirect" title="Diya (light)">diyas</a>, candles and lanterns<sup id="cite_ref-melton252_8-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-melton252-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> 
Hindus, in particular, have a ritual oil bath at dawn on each day of the festival.<sup id="cite_ref-YustJohnson2006_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-YustJohnson2006-108">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> Diwali is also marked with fireworks and the decoration of floors with <a href="/wiki/Rangoli" title="Rangoli">rangoli</a> designs. Food is a major focus with families partaking in feasts and sharing <a href="/wiki/Mithai_(confectionery)" class="mw-redirect" title="Mithai (confectionery)">mithai</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-jgl_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jgl-35">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> The festival is an annual homecoming and bonding period not only for families,<sup id="cite_ref-dhcd_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dhcd-33">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Suzanne_Barchers_2013_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Suzanne_Barchers_2013-109">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> but also for communities and associations, particularly those in urban areas, which will organise activities, events and gatherings.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEChristopher_H._JohnsonSimon_TeuscherDavid_Warren_Sabean2011300–01_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEChristopher_H._JohnsonSimon_TeuscherDavid_Warren_Sabean2011300–01-110">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEManju_N._Shah199541–44_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEManju_N._Shah199541–44-111">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> Many towns organise community parades and fairs with parades or music and dance performances in parks.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPaul_Fieldhouse2017150–51_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPaul_Fieldhouse2017150–51-112">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> Some Hindus, Jains and Sikhs will send Diwali greeting cards to family near and far during the festive season, occasionally with boxes of Indian confectionery.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPaul_Fieldhouse2017150–51_112-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPaul_Fieldhouse2017150–51-112">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Diwali is a post-harvest festival celebrating the bounty following the arrival of the monsoon in the subcontinent.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDarra_Goldstein2015222–23_89-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDarra_Goldstein2015222–23-89">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> Depending on the region, celebrations include prayers before one or more Hindu deities, the most common being Lakshmi.<sup id="cite_ref-dhcd_33-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dhcd-33">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Suzanne_Barchers_2013_109-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Suzanne_Barchers_2013-109">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> According to David Kinsley, an Indologist and scholar of Indian religious traditions particularly in relation to goddess worship, Lakshmi symbolises three virtues: wealth and prosperity, fertility and abundant crops, as well as good fortune.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavid_Kinsley198833–34_107-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavid_Kinsley198833–34-107">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> Merchants seek Lakshmi's blessings in their ventures and will ritually close their accounting year during Diwali.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavid_Kinsley198833–34_107-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavid_Kinsley198833–34-107">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> Fertility motifs appear in agricultural offerings brought before Lakshmi by farming families, who give thanks for the recent harvests and seek her blessings for prosperous future crops.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavid_Kinsley198833–34_107-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavid_Kinsley198833–34-107">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> A symbolic piece of traditional fertiliser, a dried piece of cow dung, is included in the ensemble in Odisha and Deccan region villages, an agricultural motif according to Kinsley.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavid_Kinsley198833–34_107-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavid_Kinsley198833–34-107">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> Another aspect of the festival is remembering the ancestors.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Rituals and preparations for Diwali begin days or weeks in advance, typically after the festival of Dusshera that precedes Diwali by about 20 days.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOm_Lata_Bahadur200691_78-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOm_Lata_Bahadur200691-78">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> The festival formally begins two days before the night of Diwali, and ends two days thereafter. Each day has the following rituals and significance:<sup id="cite_ref-tp_32-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tp-32">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Dhanteras_.28Day_1.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Dhanteras_(Day_1)">Dhanteras (Day 1)</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Dhanteras" title="Dhanteras">Dhanteras</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:DiwaliOilLampCrop.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/DiwaliOilLampCrop.JPG/220px-DiwaliOilLampCrop.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="183" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/DiwaliOilLampCrop.JPG/330px-DiwaliOilLampCrop.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/DiwaliOilLampCrop.JPG/440px-DiwaliOilLampCrop.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1902" data-file-height="1579" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:DiwaliOilLampCrop.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Dhanteras starts off the Diwali celebrations with the lighting of <i>Diya</i> lamp rows, house cleaning and floor rangoli</div></div></div>
<p><i>Dhanteras</i>, derived from <i>Dhan</i> meaning wealth and <i>teras</i> meaning thirteenth, marks the thirteenth day of the dark fortnight of Kartik and the beginning of Diwali.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETracy_Pintchman200559–60_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETracy_Pintchman200559–60-114">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> On this day, many Hindus clean their homes and business premises. They install <i>diyas</i>, small earthen oil-filled lamps that they light up for the next five days, near Lakshmi and Ganesha iconography.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETracy_Pintchman200559–60_114-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETracy_Pintchman200559–60-114">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOm_Lata_Bahadur200692–93_106-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOm_Lata_Bahadur200692–93-106">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> Women and children decorate doorways within homes and offices with rangoli, colourful designs made from rice flour, flower petals and coloured sand,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPaul_Fieldhouse2017150–51_112-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPaul_Fieldhouse2017150–51-112">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> while the boys and men decorate the roofs and walls of family homes, markets, and temples. The day also marks a major shopping day to purchase new utensils, home equipment, jewellery, firecrackers, and other items.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOm_Lata_Bahadur200692–93_106-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOm_Lata_Bahadur200692–93-106">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-tp_32-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tp-32">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Karen_Pechilis_2007_pp_273-275_80-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Karen_Pechilis_2007_pp_273-275-80">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> On the evening of Dhanteras, families offer prayers (<i>puja</i>) to Lakshmi and Ganesha, and lay offerings of puffed rice, candy toys, rice cakes and <i>batashas</i> (hollow sugar cakes).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOm_Lata_Bahadur200692–93_106-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOm_Lata_Bahadur200692–93-106">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>According to Tracy Pintchman, Dhanteras is a symbol of annual renewal, cleansing and an auspicious beginning for the next year.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETracy_Pintchman200559–60_114-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETracy_Pintchman200559–60-114">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> The term <i>"Dhan"</i> for this day also alludes to the <a href="/wiki/Ayurvedic" class="mw-redirect" title="Ayurvedic">Ayurvedic</a> icon <a href="/wiki/Dhanvantari" title="Dhanvantari">Dhanvantari</a>, the god of health and healing, who is believed to have emerged from the "churning of cosmic ocean" on the same day as Lakshmi.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETracy_Pintchman200559–60_114-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETracy_Pintchman200559–60-114">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> Some communities, particularly those active in Ayurvedic and health-related professions, pray or perform <a href="/wiki/Yajna" title="Yajna">havan</a> rituals to Dhanvantari on Dhanteras.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETracy_Pintchman200559–60_114-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETracy_Pintchman200559–60-114">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Naraka_Chaturdashi.2C_Chhoti_Diwali_.28Day_2.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Naraka_Chaturdashi,_Chhoti_Diwali_(Day_2)">Naraka Chaturdashi, Chhoti Diwali (Day 2)</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Naraka_Chaturdashi" title="Naraka Chaturdashi">Naraka Chaturdashi</a></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r889293853/mw-parser-output/.tmulti">.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbinner{display:flex;flex-direction:column}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{display:flex;flex-direction:row;clear:left;flex-wrap:wrap;width:100%;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{margin:1px;float:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .theader{clear:both;font-weight:bold;text-align:center;align-self:center;background-color:transparent;width:100%}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption{text-align:left;background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-left{text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-right{text-align:right}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .text-align-center{text-align:center}@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbinner{width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;max-width:none!important;align-items:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .trow{justify-content:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:100%!important;box-sizing:border-box;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .thumbcaption{text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:392px;max-width:392px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:248px;max-width:248px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:138px;overflow:hidden"><a href="/wiki/File:Diwali_Food_and_Celebrations_in_Sri_Lanka_Culture_and_Sights.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Diwali_Food_and_Celebrations_in_Sri_Lanka_Culture_and_Sights.jpg/246px-Diwali_Food_and_Celebrations_in_Sri_Lanka_Culture_and_Sights.jpg" decoding="async" width="246" height="138" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Diwali_Food_and_Celebrations_in_Sri_Lanka_Culture_and_Sights.jpg/369px-Diwali_Food_and_Celebrations_in_Sri_Lanka_Culture_and_Sights.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Diwali_Food_and_Celebrations_in_Sri_Lanka_Culture_and_Sights.jpg/492px-Diwali_Food_and_Celebrations_in_Sri_Lanka_Culture_and_Sights.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1124" data-file-height="632" /></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:140px;max-width:140px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:138px;overflow:hidden"><a href="/wiki/File:Hindu_diet,_meal_setting_at_festival.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Hindu_diet%2C_meal_setting_at_festival.jpg/138px-Hindu_diet%2C_meal_setting_at_festival.jpg" decoding="async" width="138" height="138" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Hindu_diet%2C_meal_setting_at_festival.jpg/207px-Hindu_diet%2C_meal_setting_at_festival.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Hindu_diet%2C_meal_setting_at_festival.jpg/276px-Hindu_diet%2C_meal_setting_at_festival.jpg 2x" data-file-width="612" data-file-height="612" /></a></div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="thumbcaption" style="text-align:left;background-color:transparent">Choti Diwali is the major shopping day for festive <i>mithai</i> (sweets)</div></div></div></div>
<p><i>Naraka Chaturdashi</i> also known as Chhoti Diwali, is the second day of festivities coinciding with the fourteenth day of the second fortnight of the lunar month. The term "chhoti" means little, while "Naraka" means hell and "Chaturdashi" means "fourteenth".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETracy_Pintchman200560–61_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETracy_Pintchman200560–61-115">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> The day and its rituals are interpreted as ways to liberate any souls from their suffering in "Naraka", or hell, as well as a reminder of spiritual auspiciousness. For some Hindus, it is a day to pray for the peace to the manes, or deified souls of one's ancestors and light their way for their journeys in the cyclic afterlife.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETracy_Pintchman200560–61,_63_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETracy_Pintchman200560–61,_63-116">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> A mythological interpretation of this festive day is the destruction of the asura (demon) <a href="/wiki/Narakasura" title="Narakasura">Narakasura</a> by Krishna, a victory that frees 16,000 imprisoned princesses kidnapped by Narakasura.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETracy_Pintchman200560–61_115-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETracy_Pintchman200560–61-115">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Naraka Chaturdashi is also a major day for purchasing festive foods, particularly sweets. A variety of sweets are prepared using flour, semolina, rice, chickpea flour, dry fruit pieces powders or paste, milk solids (<i>mawa</i> or <i>khoya</i>) and clarified butter (<i>ghee</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDarra_Goldstein2015222–23_89-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDarra_Goldstein2015222–23-89">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> According to Goldstein, these are then shaped into various forms, such as laddus, barfis, halwa, kachoris, shrikhand, and sandesh, rolled and stuffed delicacies, such as karanji, shankarpali, maladu, susiyam, pottukadalai. Sometimes these are wrapped with edible silver foil (<a href="/wiki/Vark" title="Vark">vark</a>). Confectioners and shops create Diwali-themed decorative displays, selling these in large quantities, which are stocked for home celebrations to welcome guests and as gifts.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDarra_Goldstein2015222–23_89-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDarra_Goldstein2015222–23-89">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOm_Lata_Bahadur200692–93_106-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOm_Lata_Bahadur200692–93-106">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> Families also prepare homemade delicacies for Lakshmi Pujan, regarded as the main day of Diwali.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDarra_Goldstein2015222–23_89-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDarra_Goldstein2015222–23-89">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> Chhoti Diwali is also a day for visiting friends, business associates and relatives, and exchanging gifts.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOm_Lata_Bahadur200692–93_106-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOm_Lata_Bahadur200692–93-106">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>This day is commonly celebrated as Diwali in <a href="/wiki/Tamil_Nadu" title="Tamil Nadu">Tamil Nadu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Goa" title="Goa">Goa</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Karnataka" title="Karnataka">Karnataka</a>. Traditionally, Marathi people and South Indian Hindus receive an oil massage from the elders in the family on the day and then take a ritual bath, all before sunrise.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOm_Lata_Bahadur200693–94_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOm_Lata_Bahadur200693–94-117">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> Many visit their favourite Hindu temple.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHillary_Rodrigues201629_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHillary_Rodrigues201629-118">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Lakshmi_Puja_.28Day_3.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Lakshmi_Puja_(Day_3)">Lakshmi Puja (Day 3)</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Lakshmi_Puja" title="Lakshmi Puja">Lakshmi Puja</a></div>
<p>The third day is the height of the festival,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStephen_Jacobs201026_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStephen_Jacobs201026-119">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> and coincides with the last day of the dark fortnight of the lunar month. This is the day when Hindu, Jain and Sikh temples and homes are aglow with lights, thereby making it the "festival of lights". The word Deepawali comes from the word the <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a> word deep, which means an Indian lantern/lamp.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETracy_Pintchman200561–62_40-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETracy_Pintchman200561–62-40">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Mead2008_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mead2008-120">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The youngest members in the family visit their elders, such as grandparents and other senior members of the community, on this day. Small business owners give gifts or special bonus payments to their employees between Dhanteras and Diwali.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOm_Lata_Bahadur200693–94_117-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOm_Lata_Bahadur200693–94-117">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEConstance_Jones2011254_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConstance_Jones2011254-121">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> Shops either do not open or close early on this day allowing employees to enjoy family time. Shopkeepers and small operations perform <i>puja</i> rituals in their office premises. Unlike some other festivals, the Hindu typically do not fast during Diwali and Lakshmi Puja, rather they feast and share the bounties of the season at their workplaces, community centres, temples and homes.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOm_Lata_Bahadur200693–94_117-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOm_Lata_Bahadur200693–94-117">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Diwali_Festival.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Diwali_Festival.jpg/220px-Diwali_Festival.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Diwali_Festival.jpg/330px-Diwali_Festival.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Diwali_Festival.jpg/440px-Diwali_Festival.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="800" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Diwali_Festival.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Lighting candle and clay lamp in their house and at temples during Diwali night</div></div></div>
<p>As the evening approaches, celebrants will wear new clothes or their best outfits, teenage girls and women, in particular, wear saris and jewellery.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOm_Lata_Bahadur200694–95_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOm_Lata_Bahadur200694–95-122">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> At dusk, family members gather for the Lakshmi Puja,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOm_Lata_Bahadur200694–95_122-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOm_Lata_Bahadur200694–95-122">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup> although prayers will also be offered to other deities, such as Ganesha, Saraswati, Rama, Lakshmana, Sita, Hanuman, or Kubera.<sup id="cite_ref-tp_32-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tp-32">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> The lamps from the puja ceremony are then used to light more earthenware lamps, which are placed in rows along the parapets of temples and houses,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOm_Lata_Bahadur200697–98_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOm_Lata_Bahadur200697–98-123">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> while some <i>diyas</i> are set adrift on rivers and streams.<sup id="cite_ref-ReferenceA_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ReferenceA-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKlaus_K._Klostermaier201459,_69_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKlaus_K._Klostermaier201459,_69-125">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> After the <i>puja</i>, people go outside and celebrate by lighting up <i>patakhe</i> (fireworks) together, and then share a family feast and <i><a href="/wiki/South_Asian_sweets" class="mw-redirect" title="South Asian sweets">mithai</a></i> (sweets, desserts).<sup id="cite_ref-tp_32-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tp-32">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The <i>puja</i> and rituals in the Bengali Hindu community focus on <a href="/wiki/Kali" title="Kali">Kali</a>, the goddess of war, instead of Lakshmi.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcDermott2011183–88_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcDermott2011183–88-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEConstantina_Rhodes20101–2_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConstantina_Rhodes20101–2-126">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> According to Rachel Fell McDermott, a scholar of South Asian, particular Bengali, studies, in Bengal during Navaratri (Dussehra elsewhere in India) the <i><a href="/wiki/Durga_puja" class="mw-redirect" title="Durga puja">Durga puja</a></i> is the main focus, although in the eastern and north eastern states the two are synonymous, but on Diwali the focus is on the puja dedicated to Kali. These two festivals likely developed in tandem over their recent histories, states McDermott.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcDermott2011183–88_11-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcDermott2011183–88-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Textual evidence suggests that Bengali Hindus worshipped Lakshmi before the colonial era, and that the Kali puja is a more recent phenomenon.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;note 12&#93;</a></sup> Contemporary Bengali celebrations mirror those found elsewhere, with teenage boys playing with fireworks and the sharing of festive food with family, but with the <a href="/wiki/Shaktism" title="Shaktism">Shakti</a> goddess Kali as the focus.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJune_McDaniel2004253–55_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJune_McDaniel2004253–55-128">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sparkles_phuljhari_fireworks_on_DIWALI,_festival_of_lights.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Sparkles_phuljhari_fireworks_on_DIWALI%2C_festival_of_lights.jpg/220px-Sparkles_phuljhari_fireworks_on_DIWALI%2C_festival_of_lights.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="177" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Sparkles_phuljhari_fireworks_on_DIWALI%2C_festival_of_lights.jpg/330px-Sparkles_phuljhari_fireworks_on_DIWALI%2C_festival_of_lights.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Sparkles_phuljhari_fireworks_on_DIWALI%2C_festival_of_lights.jpg/440px-Sparkles_phuljhari_fireworks_on_DIWALI%2C_festival_of_lights.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2914" data-file-height="2348" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sparkles_phuljhari_fireworks_on_DIWALI,_festival_of_lights.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A child playing with <i>phulbaja</i> during Diwali.</div></div></div>
<p>On the night of Laksmi Puja, rituals across much of India are dedicated to Lakshmi to welcome her into their cleaned homes and bring prosperity and happiness for the coming year.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETracy_Pintchman200561–63_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETracy_Pintchman200561–63-129">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> While the cleaning, or painting, of the home is in part for goddess Lakshmi, it also signifies the ritual "reenactment of the cleansing, purifying action of the monsoon rains" that would have concluded in most of the Indian subcontinent.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETracy_Pintchman200561–63_129-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETracy_Pintchman200561–63-129">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> Vaishnava families recite Hindu legends of the victory of good over evil and the return of hope after despair during the Diwali nights, where the main characters may include Rama, Krishna, Vamana or one of the avatars of Vishnu, the divine husband of Lakshmi.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETracy_Pintchman200561–63_129-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETracy_Pintchman200561–63-129">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEOm_Lata_Bahadur200694–97_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOm_Lata_Bahadur200694–97-130">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> At dusk, lamps placed earlier in the inside and outside of the home are lit up to welcome Lakshmi.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStephen_Jacobs201026_119-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStephen_Jacobs201026-119">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> Family members light up firecrackers, which some interpret as a way to ward off all evil spirits and the inauspicious, as well as add to the festive mood.<sup id="cite_ref-Firecracker2_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Firecracker2-131">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Firecracker3_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Firecracker3-132">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> According to Pintchman, who quotes Raghavan, this ritual may also be linked to the tradition in some communities of paying respect to ancestors. Earlier in the season's fortnight, some welcome the souls of their ancestors to join the family for the festivities with the <i>Mahalaya</i>. The Diwali night's lights and firecrackers, in this interpretation, represent a celebratory and symbolic farewell to the departed ancestral souls.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETracy_Pintchman200563_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETracy_Pintchman200563-133">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The celebrations and rituals of the Jains and the Sikhs are similar to those of the Hindus where social and community bonds are renewed. Major temples and homes are decorated with lights, festive foods shared with all, friends and relatives remembered and visited with gifts.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEConstance_Jones2011254_121-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEConstance_Jones2011254-121">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJeffery_D._Long200926,_42_90-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJeffery_D._Long200926,_42-90">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Annakut.2C_Padwa.2C_Govardhan_puja_.28Day_4.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Annakut,_Padwa,_Govardhan_puja_(Day_4)">Annakut, Padwa, Govardhan puja (Day 4)</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Balipratipada" title="Balipratipada">Balipratipada</a></div>
<p>The day after Diwali is the first day of the bright fortnight of the luni-solar calendar.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETracy_Pintchman200563–64_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETracy_Pintchman200563–64-134">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> It is regionally called as Annakut (heap of grain), Padwa, Goverdhan puja, Bali Pratipada, Bali Padyami, Kartik Shukla Pratipada and other names.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPaul_Fieldhouse2017150–51_112-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPaul_Fieldhouse2017150–51-112">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETracy_Pintchman200563–64_134-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETracy_Pintchman200563–64-134">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> According to one tradition, the day is associated with the story of Bali's defeat at the hands of Vishnu.<sup id="cite_ref-NarayananKurup1976_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NarayananKurup1976-135">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> In another interpretation, it is thought to reference the legend of Parvati and her husband Shiva playing a game of <i>dyuta</i> (dice) on a board of twelve squares and thirty pieces, Parvati wins. Shiva surrenders his shirt and adornments to her, rendering him naked.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETracy_Pintchman200563–64_134-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETracy_Pintchman200563–64-134">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> According to Handelman and Shulman, as quoted by Pintchman, this legend is a Hindu metaphor for the cosmic process for creation and dissolution of the world through the masculine destructive power, as represented by Shiva, and the feminine procreative power, represented by Parvati, where twelve reflects the number of months in the cyclic year, while thirty are the number of days in its lunisolar month.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETracy_Pintchman200563–64_134-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETracy_Pintchman200563–64-134">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r889293853/mw-parser-output/.tmulti"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:392px;max-width:392px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:258px;max-width:258px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:170px;overflow:hidden"><a href="/wiki/File:Annakut.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Annakut.jpg/256px-Annakut.jpg" decoding="async" width="256" height="170" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Annakut.jpg/384px-Annakut.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Annakut.jpg/512px-Annakut.jpg 2x" data-file-width="604" data-file-height="401" /></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:130px;max-width:130px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:170px;overflow:hidden"><a href="/wiki/File:Lord_Krishna_and_Goverdhan_parwat_in_his_left_hand%27s_finger.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Lord_Krishna_and_Goverdhan_parwat_in_his_left_hand%27s_finger.jpg/128px-Lord_Krishna_and_Goverdhan_parwat_in_his_left_hand%27s_finger.jpg" decoding="async" width="128" height="171" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Lord_Krishna_and_Goverdhan_parwat_in_his_left_hand%27s_finger.jpg/192px-Lord_Krishna_and_Goverdhan_parwat_in_his_left_hand%27s_finger.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Lord_Krishna_and_Goverdhan_parwat_in_his_left_hand%27s_finger.jpg/256px-Lord_Krishna_and_Goverdhan_parwat_in_his_left_hand%27s_finger.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="853" /></a></div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="thumbcaption" style="text-align:left;background-color:transparent">Annakut community meals (left), Krishna holding Govardhan ritually made from cow dung, rice and flowers (right).</div></div></div></div>
<p>This day ritually celebrates the bond between the wife and husband,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilliam_D._Crump2014112–13_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilliam_D._Crump2014112–13-137">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> and in some Hindu communities, husbands will celebrate this with gifts to their wives. In other regions, parents invite a newly married daughter, or son, together with their spouses to a festive meal and give them gifts.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilliam_D._Crump2014112–13_137-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilliam_D._Crump2014112–13-137">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In some rural communities of the north, west and central regions, the fourth day is celebrated as Govardhan puja, honouring the legend of the Hindu god Krishna saving the cowherd and farming communities from incessant rains and floods triggered by Indra's anger,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilliam_D._Crump2014112–13_137-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilliam_D._Crump2014112–13-137">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> which he accomplished by lifting the Govardhan mountain. This legend is remembered through the ritual of building small mountain-like miniatures from cow dung.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilliam_D._Crump2014112–13_137-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilliam_D._Crump2014112–13-137">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> According to Kinsley, the ritual use of cow dung, a common fertiliser, is an agricultural motif and a celebration of its significance to annual crop cycles.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavid_Kinsley198833–34_107-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavid_Kinsley198833–34-107">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETracy_Pintchman200564–65_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETracy_Pintchman200564–65-138">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELodrick1987_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELodrick1987-139">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The agricultural symbolism is also observed on this day by many Hindus as Annakut, literally "mountain of food". Communities prepare over one hundred dishes from a variety of ingredients, which is then dedicated to Krishna before shared among the community. Hindu temples on this day prepare and present "mountains of sweets" to the faithful who have gathered for <i>darshan</i> (visit).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilliam_D._Crump2014112–13_137-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilliam_D._Crump2014112–13-137">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> In Gujarat, Annakut is the first day of the new year and celebrated through the purchase of essentials, or <i>sabras</i> (literally, "good things in life"), such as salt, offering prayers to Krishna and visiting temples.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilliam_D._Crump2014112–13_137-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilliam_D._Crump2014112–13-137">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Bhai_Duj.2C_Bhau-Beej_.28Day_5.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Bhai_Duj,_Bhau-Beej_(Day_5)">Bhai Duj, Bhau-Beej (Day 5)</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Bhau-beej" class="mw-redirect" title="Bhau-beej">Bhau-beej</a></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r889293853/mw-parser-output/.tmulti"/><div class="thumb tmulti tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;max-width:292px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:286px;max-width:286px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:213px;overflow:hidden"><a href="/wiki/File:Diwali_(Bhaiya_Dweej_).JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Diwali_%28Bhaiya_Dweej_%29.JPG/284px-Diwali_%28Bhaiya_Dweej_%29.JPG" decoding="async" width="284" height="213" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Diwali_%28Bhaiya_Dweej_%29.JPG/426px-Diwali_%28Bhaiya_Dweej_%29.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/82/Diwali_%28Bhaiya_Dweej_%29.JPG/568px-Diwali_%28Bhaiya_Dweej_%29.JPG 2x" data-file-width="4608" data-file-height="3456" /></a></div></div></div><div class="trow"><div class="thumbcaption" style="text-align:left;background-color:transparent">A sister ritually feeding her brother on Bhai Duj-Diwali (left), a lit rangoli (right)</div></div></div></div>
<p>The last day of the festival is called <i>Bhai Duj</i> (literally "brother's day"<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup>), <i>Bhau Beej</i>, <i>Bhai Tilak</i> or <i>Bhai Phonta</i>. It celebrates the sister-brother bond, similar in spirit to <a href="/wiki/Raksha_Bandhan" title="Raksha Bandhan">Raksha Bandhan</a> but it is the brother that travels to meet the sister and her family. This festive day is interpreted by some to symbolise Yama's sister Yamuna welcoming Yama with a <i>tilaka</i>, while others interpret it as the arrival of Krishna at his sister's, Subhadra, place after defeating Narakasura. Subhadra welcomes him with a <i>tilaka</i> on his forehead.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilliam_D._Crump2014112–13_137-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilliam_D._Crump2014112–13-137">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETracy_Pintchman200566–69_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETracy_Pintchman200566–69-141">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The day celebrates the sibling bond between brother and sister. On this day the womenfolk of the family gather, perform a puja with prayers for the well being of their brothers, then return to a ritual of feeding their brothers with their hands and receiving gifts. According to Pintchman, in some Hindu traditions the women recite tales where sisters protect their brothers from enemies that seek to cause him either bodily or spiritual harm.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETracy_Pintchman200566–69_141-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETracy_Pintchman200566–69-141">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> In historic times, this was a day in autumn when brothers would travel to meet their sisters, or invite their sister's family to their village to celebrate their sister-brother bond with the bounty of seasonal harvests.<sup id="cite_ref-tp_32-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tp-32">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The artisan Hindu and Sikh community celebrates the fourth day as the Vishwakarma puja day.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;note 13&#93;</a></sup> Vishwakarma is the presiding Hindu deity for those in architecture, building, manufacturing, textile work and crafts trades.<sup id="cite_ref-haar2009_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-haar2009-18">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;note 14&#93;</a></sup> The looms, tools of trade, machines and workplaces are cleaned and prayers offered to these livelihood means.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_traditions_and_significance">Other traditions and significance</span></h2>
<p>During the season of Diwali, numerous rural townships and villages host <i><a href="/wiki/Mela_Festival" class="mw-redirect" title="Mela Festival">melas</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-Kadowala1998_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kadowala1998-147">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> or fairs, where local producers and artisans trade produce and goods. A variety of entertainments are usually available for inhabitants of the local community to enjoy. The womenfolk, in particular, adorn themselves in colourful attire and <a href="/wiki/Mehndi" title="Mehndi">decorate</a> their hands with <a href="/wiki/Henna" title="Henna">henna</a>. Such events are also mentioned in Sikh historical records.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarjot_Oberoi1994188–90_with_footnote_96_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarjot_Oberoi1994188–90_with_footnote_96-148">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;note 15&#93;</a></sup> In the modern day, <i>Diwali mela</i> are held at college, or university, campuses or as community events by members of the Indian diaspora. At such events a variety of music, dance and arts performances, food, crafts, and cultural celebrations are featured.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFrank_Salamone2004112,_Article_on_Divali_by_Lindsey_Harlan_87-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFrank_Salamone2004112,_Article_on_Divali_by_Lindsey_Harlan-87">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Economics">Economics</span></h3>
<p>Diwali marks a major shopping period in India,<sup id="cite_ref-wsj_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wsj-13">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> and is comparable to the Christmas period in terms of consumer purchases and economic activity.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKlaus_K._Klostermaier201459_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKlaus_K._Klostermaier201459-152">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> It is traditionally a time when households purchase new clothing, home refurbishments, gifts, gold, jewellery,<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> and other large purchases particularly as the festival is dedicated to Lakshmi, the goddess of wealth and prosperity, and such purchases are considered auspicious.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> According to Rao, Diwali is one of the major festivals where rural Indians spend a significant portion of their annual income, and is a means for them to renew their relationships and social networks.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERao200171–95_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERao200171–95-157">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> Other goods that are bought in substantial quantities during Diwali include confectionery and fireworks. In 2013, about <span class="nowrap"><span style="white-space: nowrap">₹</span>25 billion</span> (US$360&#160;million) of fireworks were sold to merchants for the Diwali season, an equivalent retail value of about <span class="nowrap"><span style="white-space: nowrap">₹</span>50 billion</span> (US$720&#160;million) according to The Times of India.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">&#91;note 16&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/ASSOCHAM" title="ASSOCHAM">ASSOCHAM</a>, a trade organisation in India, forecasted that online shopping alone to be over <span class="nowrap"><span style="white-space: nowrap">₹</span>300 billion</span> (US$4.3&#160;billion) over the 2017 Diwali season.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> About two-thirds of Indian households, according to the ASSOCHAM forecast, would spend between <span class="nowrap"><span style="white-space: nowrap">₹</span>5,000</span> (US$72) and <span class="nowrap"><span style="white-space: nowrap">₹</span>10,000</span> (US$140) to celebrate Diwali in 2017.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Politics">Politics</span></h3>
<p>Diwali has increasingly attracted cultural exchanges, becoming occasions for politicians and religious leaders worldwide to meet Hindu or Indian origin citizens, diplomatic staff or neighbours. Many participate in other socio-political events as a symbol of support for diversity and inclusiveness. The Catholic dicastery <a href="/wiki/Pontifical_Council_for_Interreligious_Dialogue" title="Pontifical Council for Interreligious Dialogue">Pontifical Council for Interreligious Dialogue</a>, founded as Secretariat for non-Christians by Pope Paul VI, began sending official greetings and Pope's message to the Hindus on Diwali in the mid-1990s.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarbato201793–97_164-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarbato201793–97-164">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">&#91;note 17&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Many governments encourage or sponsor Diwali-related festivities in their territories. For example, the Singaporean government, in association with the Hindu Endowment Board of Singapore, organises many cultural events during Diwali every year.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> National and civic leaders such as <a href="/wiki/Prince_Charles" class="mw-redirect" title="Prince Charles">Prince Charles</a> have attended Diwali celebrations at prominent Hindu temples in the UK, such as the <a href="/wiki/Neasden_Temple" class="mw-redirect" title="Neasden Temple">Swaminarayan Temple in Neasden</a>, using the occasion to highlight contributions of the Hindu community to British society.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> Since 2009, Diwali has been celebrated every year at <a href="/wiki/10_Downing_Street" title="10 Downing Street">10 Downing Street</a>, the residence of the British Prime Minister.<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Diwali was first celebrated in the <a href="/wiki/White_House" title="White House">White House</a> by <a href="/wiki/George_W._Bush" title="George W. Bush">George W. Bush</a> in 2003 and was given official status by the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">United States Congress</a> in 2007.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a> became the first president to personally attend Diwali at the White House in 2009. On the eve of his first visit to India as President of the United States, Obama released an official statement sharing his best wishes with "those celebrating Diwali".<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Every year during Diwali, Indian forces approach their <a href="/wiki/Pakistani" class="mw-redirect" title="Pakistani">Pakistani</a> counterparts at the border bearing gifts of traditional Indian confectionery, a gesture that is returned in kind by the Pakistani soldiers who give Pakistani sweets to the Indian soldiers.<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">&#91;note 18&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Issues">Issues</span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Air_pollution">Air pollution</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;"><a href="/wiki/File:AQI_2015_data_PM2.5_and_PM10_air_pollution_and_Diwali,_Delhi_India.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/AQI_2015_data_PM2.5_and_PM10_air_pollution_and_Diwali%2C_Delhi_India.jpg/310px-AQI_2015_data_PM2.5_and_PM10_air_pollution_and_Diwali%2C_Delhi_India.jpg" decoding="async" width="310" height="255" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/AQI_2015_data_PM2.5_and_PM10_air_pollution_and_Diwali%2C_Delhi_India.jpg/465px-AQI_2015_data_PM2.5_and_PM10_air_pollution_and_Diwali%2C_Delhi_India.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/AQI_2015_data_PM2.5_and_PM10_air_pollution_and_Diwali%2C_Delhi_India.jpg/620px-AQI_2015_data_PM2.5_and_PM10_air_pollution_and_Diwali%2C_Delhi_India.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1906" data-file-height="1566" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:AQI_2015_data_PM2.5_and_PM10_air_pollution_and_Diwali,_Delhi_India.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The AQI PM<sub>2.5</sub> and PM<sub>10</sub> data for air quality from 1 August to 31 December 2015 in Delhi, India.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup> The 5-day Diwali festival in 2015 was observed from 9 to 13 November.</div></div></div>
<p>The tradition of annual Diwali fireworks has caused widespread coverage in Indian media, where debate has centred on air quality within Indian cities in autumn and winter and the role the fireworks play. On 9 October 2017, the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_India" title="Supreme Court of India">Supreme Court of India</a> banned the sale, but not use, of fireworks in Delhi during the Diwali season,<sup id="cite_ref-veconomist_179-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-veconomist-179">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup> with the assumption that banning the use of fireworks would substantially improve the air quality of Delhi. Critics stated that the ruling was judicial over-reach and a bias against Hindu culture, while supporters stated that it would be beneficial to public health.<sup id="cite_ref-veconomist_179-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-veconomist-179">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Scholars have stated that many factors contribute to the poor air quality in Delhi, and northern India, that accompanies the harvest festival of Diwali. According to Jethva and others, the post-monsoon custom is to prepare the crop fields by deliberately burning the residual stubble between October and November.<sup id="cite_ref-Jethva2018_180-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jethva2018-180">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Singh2017_181-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Singh2017-181">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup> As crop productivity per hectare has increased with mechanised harvesting, this has led to the practice becoming more widespread in the northern and northwestern regions of India in the months when Diwali is observed.<sup id="cite_ref-Jethva2018_180-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jethva2018-180">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> The smoke from the burning of the fields is carried by seasonal winds over the floodplain, where it is inverted by the colder winds and spread throughout the region for much of the winter. Other contributors to the poor air quality include daily vehicular and industrial activity along with the burning of other biomass.<sup id="cite_ref-Jethva2018_180-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jethva2018-180">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Singh2017_181-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Singh2017-181">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A study of residential areas of Delhi in 2006-08, found that concentration of <a href="/wiki/Particulate_matter" class="mw-redirect" title="Particulate matter">particulate matter</a> as well as <a href="/wiki/Sulphur_dioxide" class="mw-redirect" title="Sulphur dioxide">sulphur dioxide</a> increased on the day of Diwali relative to other days of the month and that the increase could be exacerbated by prevailing meteorological conditions; there was also a correlated increase in <a href="/wiki/Noise_pollution" title="Noise pollution">ambient noise levels</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup> Another study of air pollution in Delhi found that the PM<sub>2.5</sub> levels in 2015 and 2016 did rise over Diwali, but these higher levels were "a result of contribution from fireworks on the Diwali night, trans-regional movement of pollutants due to crop residue burning, low wind speed, and high humidity". The authors concluded that the contribution of the festival fireworks could lead to a 1.3% increase in the non-carcinogenic hazard index.<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup> Other studies have stated that the fireworks of Diwali produce particulates and pollutants with a decay-life time of about one day.<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">&#91;note 19&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Burn_injuries">Burn injuries</span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Fireworks_safety" class="mw-redirect" title="Fireworks safety">Fireworks safety</a></div>
<p>The use of fireworks also causes an increase in the number of burn injuries in India during Diwali. One particular firework called <i>anar</i> (fountain) has been found to be responsible for 65% of such injuries, with adults being the typical victims. Most of the injuries sustained are Group I type burns (minor) requiring only outpatient care.<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
